Book and Newspaper Reviews

Guangxi Folk Literature 238

— Tang Zengyang

Edited by Qu Duren

Most of the systematically organized collections of folk literature published by major publishers at sea today are taken from eastern Guangdong or remote Sichuan. There has been no collection of "Guangxi Folk Literature," which is very remote. Therefore, scholars who study folk literature generally regret not being able to understand the customs and temperament of the people of Guangxi. Recently, the folk literature of this region, compiled by Mr. Qu, is active before our eyes, making up for the shortcomings of Chinese people not being able to understand each other's folk customs. In the editor's preface, he described this point very thoroughly: "Although Guangxi also occupies a piece of China's territory, because of its remote location, compared to the journey in the minds of most Chinese people, mentioning Guangxi seems even less convenient than mentioning England or America. In fact, there are fewer Chinese people who go to Guangxi than to England or America. Guangxi is buried in the corners of most Chinese people's brain banks along with words like the Sahara Desert and the North and South Poles." This is a very obvious fact, because people who live in the central regions, except for those who have special circumstances, rarely run to a corner of Guangxi without a purpose. But because it is buried by most people, when they occasionally think of it—Guangxi—"they also feel the same mysterious interest as the Sahara Desert and the North and South Poles, and all kinds of illusions such as miasmas spreading across the wilderness, Miao and Yao people in groups, green faces and sharp teeth... are linked together. The nickname 'Secret Western Country' given to Guangxi by most Chinese people expresses this meaning. Guangxi is buried by the Chinese people because of its remote location, and because it has always been buried, it has been felt by the Chinese people with a mysterious interest. Therefore, Guangxi also has a tendency to be re-excavated from the corners of the Chinese people's brain banks for research." This is indeed an extremely good phenomenon. Therefore, when we study the folk literature left in various places to this day, we must first start with the folk libraries in these border areas that are not easily accessible. This is also the concept of "generality," and things that are considered difficult to obtain in the eyes of the Chinese are always particularly precious. The so-called literature is, of course, the same. In fact, this concept is not wrong, perhaps it is learned from experience.

The editor is very careful and divides the contents of this book into six parts. The first and second parts are both about love. Because the meaning of "sex" is relatively heavy in the second part, the editor, not afraid of the trouble, specially opened up a column for it, that is, dividing it into "Love" (upper) and "Love" (lower). The third part, "Women," describes the lives of women. The fourth part, "Farmers," describes rural life. The fifth part is "Nursery Rhymes," and the sixth part is "Miscellaneous." Such a systematic classification can also be regarded as a unique feature of this compilation, making readers feel extra convenient when chanting and clearly understand the content described, for example:

If brother has the heart and sister has the heart,

We are not afraid of high mountains and deep waters,

There are still people walking on high mountains,

There are ferries in deep waters.

Such a love song, rich in clear and simple beauty, and containing profound implications, is indeed rare in ordinary published collections of love songs. It is truly a beautiful and moving good song.

From this collection, we see the psychology of the people of Guangxi. The era is very far away, probably because of the inconvenience of transportation, and new ideas cannot be universally imported, so to this day, it still retains some old-fashioned thinking, such as:

Rose flowers bloom with rose fragrance,

Swaying and swinging to school,

Husband, ah husband,

Do your homework well,

Don't pick flowers on the road,

There are begonias in your own room.

From this, we can see the expectations of Guangxi women for men. Although this poem is about "love," it also crisscrosses with an upward consciousness, urging the loved one to work hard, but the purpose of the effort is still to make her husband a successful candidate in the imperial examination. However, this concept cannot be generalized, such as:

Husband, you don't have to boast,

How many people enjoy prosperity by studying,

Although my husband is a farmer's son,

He is already at home in the fields at dusk.

This love song also represents another kind of psychology of Guangxi women. This psychology is also because China is a country that values agriculture, so from the most ancient times to the present, there is still a concept of believing in the life of farmers. In this collection of "Folk Literature," there are particularly many descriptions of this kind, such as:

It's really pitiful,

There is no quilt on the bed and no edge on the mat,

There is also no firewood, rice, oil, or salt,

No fish or meat is seen,

Everyone comes to pay New Year's greetings,

The child cries for his mother,

The stomach is as bitter as Coptis chinensis.

This song completely reveals the weakness of the economic power of the people of Guangxi, and it is so that we are moved by listening to it. This is the unique value of "Folk Literature."

Worry comes and goes, worry becomes more and more,

Always guarding against thieves, never resting,

Guarding the night and watching the day,

I wish to hear the early singing of the song of peace.

Readers will generally know that there are really as many bandits in Guangxi as there are tangled hemp. Even these poor farmers are frightened by it, but they also lack the ability to govern and defend themselves. This is a fact. As long as we sing the hopeful cry of this "I wish to hear the early singing of the song of peace," we can certainly believe it without any doubt. At the same time, they have reached the "harvesting period," which is so joyful, obviously seeing the light in "suffering," "In the sixtieth month of the sky, it is the harvesting period, the husband and wife go out and laugh, I will do the threshing and you will do the cutting, now we are not afraid of the hunger of the children." Under such circumstances, it is natural that songs like the above are evoked.

There are also many "Nursery Rhymes" that are worth chanting. Now, I will cite one or two to share with those who have the same interests.

The moon is bright,

Shining into the sister-in-law's room,

The elder brother studies until midnight,

The sister-in-law splits hemp until dawn.

In the senses of children, such an awareness of men diligently reading and women diligently weaving is deeply imprinted. This is also worth considering. There are many other "Nursery Rhymes," but due to the length of the article, it is a pity that I cannot cite them all.

The last part is "Miscellaneous," and there are also many good songs that reveal true feelings. I can only let the readers appreciate them for themselves.

Manuscript Guidelines for Book and Newspaper Reviews 239

(1) The text is limited to introducing the latest publications at home and abroad. In addition to literary works, all political, economic, legal, scientific, educational, and other books and newspapers are included in the introduction, but boring books and newspapers will not be introduced.

(2) Pictures are limited to the latest works of new writers at home and abroad and illustrations related to the text.

(3) Manuscripts on textual research or research on ancient Chinese books will not be published.

(4) Pictures and photos are limited to those related to the text.

(5) Each article should not exceed three thousand words at most.

(6) Do not write on both sides of the manuscript paper.
